     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Southeastern Conference
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       SEC
      </b>
      ) is an American college
      <a href="/wiki/Athletic_conference" title="Athletic conference">
       athletic conference
      </a>
      whose member institutions are located primarily in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
       Southern
      </a>
      part of the United States. The conference is headquartered in
      <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">
       Birmingham, Alabama
      </a>
      . The SEC participates in the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">
       National Collegiate Athletic Association
      </a>
      (NCAA)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_I_(NCAA)" title="Division I (NCAA)">
       Division I
      </a>
      in sports competitions; for
      <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">
       football
      </a>
      , it is part of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Football_Bowl_Subdivision" title="Football Bowl Subdivision">
       Football Bowl Subdivision
      </a>
      (FBS), formerly known as Division I-A. Regarded as one of the most accomplished sports conferences in terms of its winning reputation, the SEC is also highly successful financially. The conference consistently leads most others in revenue distribution to its members, including an SEC record $455.8 million for the 2014-15 fiscal year,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which was a sizable increase over the $292.8 million for the 2013-14 fiscal year,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEC-08Rev_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SEC-08Rev-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      largely due to the revenue from the introduction of the
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Network" title="SEC Network">
       SEC Network
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC was also the first NCAA Division I conference to hold a championship game (and award a subsequent title) for
      <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">
       college football
      </a>
      and was one of the founding members of the
      <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">
       Bowl Championship Series
      </a>
      (BCS). The current SEC commissioner is
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Slive" title="Michael Slive">
       Michael Slive
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The conference sponsors team championships in nine men's sports and twelve women's sports.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Member_universities">
       Member universities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Member universities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_members">
       Current members
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Current members">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The SEC consists of 14 member institutions located within the borders of 11 contiguous states. Listed in alphabetical order, these 11 states within the SEC's geographical footprint are
      <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">
       Alabama
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">
       Arkansas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
       Florida
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">
       Georgia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">
       Kentucky
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">
       Louisiana
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">
       Mississippi
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">
       Missouri
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">
       South Carolina
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">
       Tennessee
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
       Texas
      </a>
      . The geographic domain of the conference is predominantly within the Southeastern and Southern United States (with the notable exception of Missouri), and stretches from Texas in the west to South Carolina in the east and from Missouri in the north to Florida in the south.
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC is divided into Eastern and Western Divisions although the divisional labels are misnomers given Missouri's Eastern Division alignment while being located in the west geographically, and Auburn's Western Division alignment despite being located considerably further east than Missouri and Vanderbilt. These divisional groupings are applied exclusively to football and baseball as well as their scheduling and standings.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since July 1, 2012, the 14 members of the SEC are:

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">

       </a>

        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_former_members">
       Founding and former members
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Founding and former members">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The SEC was established on December 8 and 9, 1932, when the thirteen members of the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Conference" title="Southern Conference">
       Southern Conference
      </a>
      located west and south of the
      <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">
       Appalachian Mountains
      </a>
      left to form their own conference. Ten of the thirteen founding members have remained in the conference since its inception: the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">
       University of Alabama
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Auburn_University" title="Auburn University">
       Auburn University
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">
       University of Florida
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">
       University of Georgia
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kentucky" title="University of Kentucky">
       University of Kentucky
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_University" title="Louisiana State University">
       Louisiana State University
      </a>
      ("LSU"), the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mississippi" title="University of Mississippi">
       University of Mississippi
      </a>
      ("Ole Miss"),
      <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_University" title="Mississippi State University">
       Mississippi State University
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tennessee" title="University of Tennessee">
       University of Tennessee
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">
       Vanderbilt University
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The other charter members were:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Sewanee:_The_University_of_the_South" title="Sewanee: The University of the South">
        The University of the South
       </a>
       ("Sewanee") left the SEC on December 13, 1940, and later de-emphasized varsity sports.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sewanee1_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sewanee1-9">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         6
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is currently a member of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_III_(NCAA)" title="Division III (NCAA)">
        Division III
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_Athletic_Association" title="Southern Athletic Association">
        Southern Athletic Association
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">
        Georgia Institute of Technology
       </a>
       ("Georgia Tech") left the SEC in 1964. In 1975, it became a founding member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Metro_Conference" title="Metro Conference">
        Metro Conference
       </a>
       , one of the predecessors to today's
       <a href="/wiki/Conference_USA" title="Conference USA">
        Conference USA
       </a>
       . Georgia Tech competed in the Metro Conference in all sports except football, in which it was independent. In 1978, Georgia Tech joined another Southern Conference offshoot, the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Conference" title="Atlantic Coast Conference">
        Atlantic Coast Conference
       </a>
       , for all sports, where it has remained ever since.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">
        Tulane University
       </a>
       left the SEC in 1966. Along with Georgia Tech, it was a charter member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Metro_Conference" title="Metro Conference">
        Metro Conference
       </a>
       . Unlike Tech, however, Tulane remained in the Metro Conference until it merged with the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Midwest_Conference" title="Great Midwest Conference">
        Great Midwest Conference
       </a>
       in 1995 to form Conference USA. Tulane remained an independent in football until C-USA began football competition in 1996. Tulane left C-USA in 2014 for the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Athletic_Conference" title="American Athletic Conference">
        American Athletic Conference
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1990_expansion">
       1990 expansion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1990 expansion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Conference" title="Southwest Conference">
       Southwest Conference
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1990, the SEC expanded from ten to twelve member universities with the addition of the
      <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_Razorbacks" title="Arkansas Razorbacks">
       Arkansas Razorbacks
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Gamecocks" title="South Carolina Gamecocks">
       South Carolina Gamecocks
      </a>
      . The two new teams began SEC competition with the 19911992 basketball season.
     </p>
     <p>
      At the same time, the SEC organized competition for some sports into two divisions. The Western Division comprised six of the seven member schools in the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Time_Zone" title="Central Time Zone">
       Central Time Zone
      </a>
      , while the Eastern Division comprised the five member schools in the
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">
       Eastern Time Zone
      </a>
      plus Vanderbilt, which is in the Central Time Zone but was placed in the Eastern Division to preserve its rivalry with Tennessee. Initially, the divisional format was used in football, baseball, and men's basketball. The divisional format was dropped for men's basketball following the 2011-2012 season.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following expansion, the SEC was the first conference to receive permission from the NCAA to sponsor an annual
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Championship_Game" title="SEC Championship Game">
       football championship game
      </a>
      , featuring the winners of the conference's Eastern and Western divisions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-about-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/1992_SEC_Championship_Game" title="1992 SEC Championship Game">
       1992
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1993_SEC_Championship_Game" title="1993 SEC Championship Game">
       1993
      </a>
      championship games were held at
      <a href="/wiki/Legion_Field" title="Legion Field">
       Legion Field
      </a>
      in Birmingham, and all championship games from
      <a href="/wiki/1994_SEC_Championship_Game" title="1994 SEC Championship Game">
       1994
      </a>
      onward have been held at the
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Dome" title="Georgia Dome">
       Georgia Dome
      </a>
      in Atlanta.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-about-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="2012_expansion">
       2012 expansion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2012 expansion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9312_Southeastern_Conference_realignment" title="201012 Southeastern Conference realignment">
       201012 Southeastern Conference realignment
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      On September 25, 2011, the SEC Presidents and Chancellors, acting unanimously, announced that
      <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">
       Texas A&amp;M University
      </a>
      would join the SEC effective July 1, 2012, with Texas A&amp;M to begin competition in nineteen of the twenty sports sponsored by the SEC during the 201213 academic year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On November 6, 2011 the SEC commissioner announced that the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">
       University of Missouri
      </a>
      would also join the SEC on July 1, 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For football, Texas A&amp;M was scheduled to compete in the Western Division, and Missouri in the Eastern Division.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washpo-secnewmember20111228_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-washpo-secnewmember20111228-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhamnews28dec2011_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bhamnews28dec2011-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espn-28dec2011_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-espn-28dec2011-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sportingnewsdecember282011_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sportingnewsdecember282011-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Texas A&amp;M and Missouri both left the
      <a href="/wiki/Big_12_Conference" title="Big 12 Conference">
       Big 12 Conference
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commissioners">

      <span class="mw-editsection">

        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Academics_and_SECU">
       Academics and SECU
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Academics and SECU">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_formation_of_SECU_and_SEC_academic_network">
       The formation of SECU and SEC academic network
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The formation of SECU and SEC academic network">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_(SECNAV)_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus,_left,_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot,_Uga.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1848" data-file-width="2784" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg/220px-US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg/330px-US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg/440px-US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_%28SECNAV%29_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus%2C_left%2C_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot%2C_Uga.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_101019-N-5549O-115_Secretary_of_the_Navy_(SECNAV)_the_Honorable_Ray_Mabus,_left,_is_presented_a_statue_of_University_of_Georgia_mascot,_Uga.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Dr.
        <a href="/wiki/Michael_F._Adams" title="Michael F. Adams">
         Michael F. Adams
        </a>
        (pictured at right) was President of the
        <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">
         University of Georgia
        </a>
        and chaired the SEC Presidents and Chancellors during the formation of SECU in 2005.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Under the leadership of
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_F._Adams" title="Michael F. Adams">
       Michael F. Adams
      </a>
      the then President of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">
       University of Georgia
      </a>
      and chair of SEC Presidents and Chancellors, the member institutions of the Southeastern Conference joined forces in 2005 to form the SEC Academic Consortium (SECAC), a collaborative endeavor designed to promote research, scholarship, and achievement amongst the universities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2011, the SEC Academic Consortium was relocated to the SEC headquarters in
      <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">
       Birmingham, Alabama
      </a>
      , from its original home on the campus of the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arkansas" title="University of Arkansas">
       University of Arkansas
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Fayetteville,_Arkansas" title="Fayetteville, Arkansas">
       Fayetteville, Arkansas
      </a>
      and was renamed SECU. The SECU rebranded its mission to better serve as a means through which the collaborative academic endeavors and achievements of Southeastern Conference universities would be promoted and advanced. The SECU's goals included highlighting the endeavors and achievements of SEC
      <a href="/wiki/Faculty_(academic_staff)" title="Faculty (academic staff)">
       faculty
      </a>
      , students and its universities; advancing the academic reputation of SEC universities; identifying and preparing future leaders for high-level service in
      <a href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">
       academia
      </a>
      ; increasing the amount and type of
      <a href="/wiki/Study_abroad" title="Study abroad">
       study abroad
      </a>
      opportunities available for students; and providing opportunities for collaboration among SEC university personnel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">
       Big Ten Conference
      </a>
      has a similar program called the
      <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Institutional_Cooperation" title="Committee on Institutional Cooperation">
       Committee on Institutional Cooperation
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC Symposium component of SECU was crafted by
      <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">
       Vanderbilt University
      </a>
      Chancellor
      <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_S._Zeppos" title="Nicholas S. Zeppos">
       Nicholas S. Zeppos
      </a>
      , who at the time was the Vice President of the SEC Executive Committee and liaison to SECU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In an interview with Dr. Zeppos about the formation of the SECU he noted, "that the member institutions of the Southeastern Conference are committed to a shared mission of fostering research, scholarship, and achievement. The SEC Symposium represents a platform to connect, collaborate and promote a productive dialogue that will span disciplinary and institutional boundaries and allow us to work together for the betterment of society."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC Academic Network was created in 2009 in partnership with
      <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">
       ESPN
      </a>
      . The SEC Academic Network was an online library of institutionally produced videos featuring academic initiatives and stories from all Southeastern Conference institutions. The SEC Academic Network was officially merged into the SECU operation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="SECU_academic_programs">
       SECU academic programs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: SECU academic programs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-move" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="50" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Merge-arrow.svg/50px-Merge-arrow.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Merge-arrow.svg/75px-Merge-arrow.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Merge-arrow.svg/100px-Merge-arrow.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
        </div>

       </td>
      </tr>

     <p>
      There are several programs that have been implemented under SECU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC Academic Leadership Development Program
      </b>
      seeks to identify, prepare, and advance academic leaders for roles within SEC institutions and beyond. It has two components, a university-level program and two three-day, SEC-wide workshops held on specified campuses for all participants.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC Faculty Achievement and Professor of the Year Awards
      </b>
      recognize faculty with outstanding records in research and scholarship. There is one winner per campus and one overall winner for the Conference.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC Faculty Travel Grant Program
      </b>
      is intended to enhance collaboration that stimulates scholarly initiatives between SEC universities. The program offers faculty from each SEC university the opportunity to travel to other SEC universities to develop grant proposals and conduct research.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC College Tour
      </b>
      occurs each spring, and administrators from all SEC universities participate in events intended to introduce SEC universities to students, parents, and high school counselors from outside of the Southeast region.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC Symposium
      </b>
      is an academic conference-type event intended to address a scholarly issue in an area of strength represented by all SEC universities. Held in
      <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">
       Atlanta
      </a>
      , this marquee event puts on display the research and innovation of SEC institutions for an audience of academicians, government officials, grant funding agents, and other stakeholders.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2013, the SEC Symposium was organized and led by the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">
       University of Georgia
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia#UGA_Bioenergy_Systems_Research_Institute" title="University of Georgia">
       UGA Bioenergy Systems Research Institute
      </a>
      . The topic of the Symposium was titled, the "Impact of the Southeast in the World's Renewable Energy Future".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC Cooperative Education Abroad Agreement
      </b>
      provides opportunities for students from all SEC universities to access international programs offered at other SEC universities. As part of a renewable agreement, Italian engineering students from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politecnico_di_Torino" title="Politecnico di Torino">
       Politecnico di Torino
      </a>
      (PdT) have the opportunity to enroll at SEC universities each fall, and engineering SEC students may study there the following spring.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC MBA Case Competition
      </b>
      are graduate level competitions provide an opportunity for SEC business schools to showcase their students' skills at solving simulated, real-world problems that cover the spectrum of business disciplines (e.g., organizational dynamics, budgeting, capitalization, competitive environment, etc.). The competition is held on one SEC campus and teams of four MBA students compete against other SEC teams, the best receiving various awards and recognition. The event is generally in April.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       SEC University Collaboration Program
      </b>
      gives university personnel groups (e.g., deans of colleges, chief financial officers, etc.) a mechanism to share best practices and ideas. Regular meetings and workshops are held university campuses, the Conference office or other similar locations.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       International/Education Abroad Activities program
      </b>
      was established as a part of a renewable agreement. Italian engineering students enroll at SEC universities each fall, and engineering SEC students study in Italy each spring (i.e., February to July) at the Politecnico di Torino. In addition, by utilizing a cooperative programming agreement, students from all SEC universities have access to education abroad programs offered at other SEC universities.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spending_and_revenue">
       Spending and revenue
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Spending and revenue">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Total revenue includes ticket sales, contributions and donations, rights/licensing, student fees, school funds, and all other sources including TV income, camp income, food, and novelties. Total expenses includes coaching/staff, scholarships, buildings/grounds, maintenance, utilities and rental fees, and all other costs including recruiting, team travel, equipment and uniforms, conference dues, and insurance costs.
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
       Sports
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sports">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The Southeastern Conference sponsors championship competition in nine men's and twelve women's NCAA sanctioned sports.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Under SEC conference rules reflecting the large number of male scholarship participants in football and attempting to address
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_equity" title="Gender equity">
       gender equity
      </a>
      concerns (see also
      <a href="/wiki/Title_IX" title="Title IX">
       Title IX
      </a>
      ), each member institution is required to provide two more women's varsity sports than men's. A similar rule was recently adopted by the NCAA for all of Division I.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TitleIXrules_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TitleIXrules-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Men.27s_sponsored_sports_by_school">
       Men's sponsored sports by school
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Football">
       Football
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Football">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/2015_Southeastern_Conference_football_season" title="2015 Southeastern Conference football season">
       <img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AmericanFootball_current_event.svg/40px-AmericanFootball_current_event.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AmericanFootball_current_event.svg/60px-AmericanFootball_current_event.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/AmericanFootball_current_event.svg/80px-AmericanFootball_current_event.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
      </a>
      <i>
       For the upcoming season, see
       <a href="/wiki/2015_Southeastern_Conference_football_season" title="2015 Southeastern Conference football season">
        2015 Southeastern Conference football season
       </a>
       .
      </i>
     </p>
     <p>
      SEC teams did not play a uniform number of conference games until 1982, the number of conference games teams played ranged from 4 to 8, but most played a 6 or 7 game schedule. The league adopted a uniform 6 game schedule from 1982-1987, and added a 7th conference game from 1988 to 1991. Through this period and through the earlier years each SEC school had 5 permanent opponents, developing some traditional rivalries between schools, and the other games rotated around the other members of the conference.
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC went to an eight-game conference schedule starting in 1992, with each team playing the five other teams in their division and three opponents from the other division. The winners of the two divisions would then meet in the
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Championship_Game" title="SEC Championship Game">
       SEC Championship Game
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      From 1992 through 2002, each team had two permanent inter-divisional opponents, allowing many traditional rivalries from the pre-expansion era (such as Florida vs. Auburn, Kentucky vs. LSU, and Vanderbilt vs. Alabama) to continue. However, complaints from some league athletic directors about imbalance in the schedule (for instance, Auburn's two permanent opponents from the East were Florida and Georgia  two of the SEC's stronger football programs at the time  while Mississippi State played Kentucky and South Carolina every year) led to the SEC reducing the number of permanent inter-division opponents to one starting in the 2003 season. The TV networks televising SEC games were also pressuring for the change so attractive match-ups between non-traditional opponents would occur 2 of every 5 years instead of happening 2 of every 8 years. With the subsequent expansion to 14 teams, non-permanent cross-division opponents face each other in the regular season twice in a span of 12 years.
     </p>
     <p>
      Under the current format, each school plays a total of eight conference games, consisting of the other six teams in its division, one school from the other division on a rotating basis, and one school from the other division that it plays each year. The current scheduling arrangement was originally set to expire after the 2015 season, but the SEC presidents voted 104
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in April 2014 to keep the current format for an additional six to eight seasons beyond 2015.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEC_2016_FB_schedules_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SEC_2016_FB_schedules-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, beginning in 2016, SEC teams will be required to schedule at least one opponent each season from the other so-called "Power Five" conferences (ACC, Big Ten, Big 12, or Pac-12; games against
      <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_FBS_independent_schools" title="NCAA Division I FBS independent schools">
       football independent
      </a>
      schools also qualify, including
      <a href="/wiki/Army_Black_Knights_football" title="Army Black Knights football">
       Army
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/BYU_Cougars_football" title="BYU Cougars football">
       BYU
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_Fighting_Irish_football" title="Notre Dame Fighting Irish football">
       Notre Dame
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEC_2016_FB_schedules_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SEC_2016_FB_schedules-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEC_2016_Adjustments_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SEC_2016_Adjustments-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>

      <a href="/wiki/2013_NCAA_Division_I_FBS_football_season" title="2013 NCAA Division I FBS football season">

      </sup>
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Championship_Game">
       Championship Game
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Championship Game">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Championship_Game" title="SEC Championship Game">
       SEC Championship Game
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Championship_Game" title="SEC Championship Game">
       SEC Championship Game
      </a>
      pits the SEC Western Division representative against the Eastern Division representative in a game held after the regular season has been completed. The first two SEC Championship football games were held at
      <a href="/wiki/Legion_Field" title="Legion Field">
       Legion Field
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Birmingham,_Alabama" title="Birmingham, Alabama">
       Birmingham, Alabama
      </a>
      . Since 1994, the game has been played at the
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Dome" title="Georgia Dome">
       Georgia Dome
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">
       Atlanta
      </a>
      . The team designated as the "home" team alternates between division champions; the designation goes to the Eastern champion in even-numbered years and the Western champion in odd-numbered years. As of 2014, the Western division leads in overall wins in the championship game against the Eastern division,
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Championship_Game#Results" title="SEC Championship Game">
       12 to 11
      </a>
      .
     </p>

     <p>
      ^ The Sugar Bowl is contractually obligated to select the SEC champion if that team is not participating in the College Football Playoff. In years where the champion is unavailable the Playoff Committee will assign another SEC team to participate in the Sugar. Alternatively, in years where the Sugar hosts a playoff game the SEC Champion will be sent to the Fiesta, Cotton, or Peach Bowl if not selected for the playoff.
     </p>
     <p>
       The Big Ten and SEC will be eligible to face the ACC representative in the Orange Bowl at least three out of the eight seasons that it does not host a semifinal for the Playoff over a 12-year span. Notre Dame will be chosen the other two years if eligible.
     </p>
     <p>
       In years when the Big Ten places a team in the Orange Bowl, the Capital One Bowl will select from ACC teams remaining after the Playoff Committee and Orange Bowl make their selections.
     </p>
     <p>
       The Big Ten and ACC will switch between the Music City and TaxSlayer bowls on alternating years.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bowl_selection_procedures">
       Bowl selection procedures
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Bowl selection procedures">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="204" data-file-width="262" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          does not
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
           cite
          </a>
          any
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           references or sources
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help improve this section by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           removed
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (February 2014)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      If the SEC champion is selected to participate in the
      <a href="/wiki/BCS_National_Championship_Game" title="BCS National Championship Game">
       BCS National Championship Game
      </a>
      , the Sugar Bowl is not required to pick the SEC runner-up but may select any eligible BCS team. However, since 2006, the Sugar Bowl has selected either a division runner-up (2006 LSU, 2007 Georgia, 2010 Arkansas, and 2012 Florida) or conference runner-up (2008 Alabama, 2009 Florida), which has been the second highest ranked SEC team in the BCS standings. The Sugar Bowl was unable to select an SEC team in 2011, since two conference teams, LSU and Alabama, were ranked No. 1 and No. 2 in the final BCS standings, and thus committed to the BCS National Championship Game (which was coincidentally scheduled for New Orleans). Under BCS rules, no conference may have more than two schools play in BCS games, a provision which kept No. 5 Arkansas out of the more lucrative contests and relegated the Razorbacks to the Cotton Bowl, where they defeated #8 Kansas State. With the SEC off-limits, the January 2012 Sugar Bowl matched Big Ten at-large Michigan and ACC at-large Virginia Tech.
     </p>
     <p>
      Under SEC guidelines, unless the Sugar Bowl selects the SEC runner-up, the Capital One Bowl must then pick the SEC runner-up if that team has at least two more total wins than the next team in the selection order. 2012 runner-up Georgia was the first such team to play in the Capital One Bowl since Arkansas following the 2006 season.
     </p>
     <p>
      After those selections, the Outback Bowl and the Cotton Bowl Classic have the next choices. Due to geographical considerations, the Outback Bowl has the first choice of the remaining teams in the SEC East, while the Cotton Bowl Classic has the first choice of those left in the SEC West.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Chick-fil-A Bowl, Gator Bowl, and Music City Bowl then have the next three picks.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Liberty Bowl and BBVA Compass Bowl work together, along with the SEC office, to determine the bowls' picks.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Independence Bowl picks last. In the case that the SEC does not have enough bowl-eligible teams, another bowl eligible team will be selected instead.
     </p>
     <p>
      The SEC is presently second in BCS Bowl appearances, with twenty-one appearances, and first in all-time wins and winning percentage, with fifteen wins and a .714 winning percentage. The BCS Bowls include the Rose, Sugar, Orange, Fiesta, and the BCS National Championship Game.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the advent of the BCS National Championship Game format, the SEC is 92 in those games through the 2013 season. One SEC loss, however, was at the hands of another SEC team when the conference sent an unprecedented two teams to the 2012 National Championship game. The SEC was 20 in the games where the BCS National Championship Game was played as one of the traditional New Year's Day bowls, and since 2007 (when the game was moved to a separate contest one week later) an SEC team has participated in all eight games and has won seven. Interestingly, in games that involved only one SEC school, the SEC team was ranked No. 1 only three times going into the game (the first contest featuring Tennessee in 1998, followed by Alabama in 2009 and Auburn in 2010); the other five times the SEC team (LSU twice, Florida twice, Alabama once, Auburn once) was ranked No. 2. In the 2012 championship game, second-ranked Alabama defeated top-ranked LSU. The SEC's last loss to outside competition in a national championship game was Auburn's setback vs. Florida State in the Rose Bowl Game following the 2013 season, the last of the BCS.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Head_coach_compensation">
       Head coach compensation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

        ]

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Player_awards">
       Player awards
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Player awards">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Each year, the conference selects various
      <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference_football_individual_awards" title="Southeastern Conference football individual awards">
       individual awards
      </a>
      . In 1994, the conference began honoring former players from each school annually with the
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Football_Legends" title="SEC Football Legends">
       SEC Football Legends
      </a>
      program.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="50th_anniversary_All-Time_SEC_Team">
       50th anniversary All-Time SEC Team
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 50th anniversary All-Time SEC Team">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1982, the SEC Skywriters, a group of media covering the Southeastern Conference, selected members of their All-Time SEC Team for the first fifty years (193382) of the SEC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>

        <span>

        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>

         <b>
          Coach
         </b>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Men.27s_basketball">
       Men's basketball
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Men's basketball">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9315_Southeastern_Conference_men%27s_basketball_season" title="201415 Southeastern Conference men's basketball season">
       <img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Basketball_current_event.svg/40px-Basketball_current_event.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Basketball_current_event.svg/60px-Basketball_current_event.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Basketball_current_event.svg/80px-Basketball_current_event.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
      </a>
      <i>
       For the most recent season, see
       <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%9315_Southeastern_Conference_men%27s_basketball_season" title="201415 Southeastern Conference men's basketball season">
        201415 Southeastern Conference men's basketball season
       </a>
       .
      </i>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the 201213 season, SEC teams have played an 18-game conference schedule. Before expansion to 14 teams, the conference schedule was 16 games. Although the divisions were eliminated beginning with the 201112 season, that season's schedule was still set according to the divisional alignments, with each team facing each team from its own division twice and each team from the opposite division once. As part of the proposal by SEC head coaches that led to the scrapping of the divisional structure, a task force of four coaches and four athletic directors was set to discuss future conference scheduling. At that time, options included a revamped 16-game schedule, an 18-game schedule, or a full double round-robin of 22 conference games.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBB_changes_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MBB_changes-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, these discussions came before Texas A&amp;M and Missouri were announced in late 2011 as incoming members for the 201213 season.
     </p>
     <p>
      At the 2012 SEC spring meetings, league athletic directors adopted an 18-game conference schedule. Each school had one permanent opponent that it played home and away every season, and faced four other opponents in a home-and-home series during a given season, and then the remaining teams one each (four home, four away). The permanent opponents were Alabama-Auburn, Arkansas-Missouri, Florida-Kentucky, Georgia-South Carolina, LSU-Texas A&amp;M, Ole Miss-Mississippi State, and Tennessee-Vanderbilt. The home-and-home opponents, apart from the permanent opponent, rotated each season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The 2014 SEC spring meetings saw a change to the scheduling format. While the athletic directors voted to stay with an 18-game conference schedule, they increased the number of permanent opponents for each school from one to three. Each school will retain its permanent opponent from the 20122014 period. Further details of the new scheduling format, including the complete list of permanent opponents, have yet to be finalized.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Before the 1990 expansion to 12 schools, teams played a double round-robin, leading to an exhausting 18-game conference schedule. Not surprisingly, no team ever ran the table when the conference schedule featured 18 games; three teams went 171 (Kentucky in 1970 and 1986, LSU in 1981). During the period from 1992 to 2012 when the league slate was 16 games, Kentucky went undefeated in SEC play in 1996, 2003, and 2012 (although only the 2003 team went on to win the conference tournament).
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the return to an 18-game conference schedule following the 2012 conference expansion, two teams have gone undefeated in SEC play: Florida in 201314 and Kentucky in 201415.
     </p>
     <p>
      The new SEC coordinator of basketball officials for 2013-14 was announced as Dr. Robert "Jake" Bell. Bell is a former SEC and NCAA tournament official. He lives in Lexington, KY.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Basketball_tournament">
       Basketball tournament
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Basketball tournament">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="SEC Men's Basketball Tournament">
       SEC Men's Basketball Tournament
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/SEC_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="SEC Men's Basketball Tournament">
        SEC Men's Basketball Tournament
       </a>
      </i>
      (also known simply as the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEC_Tournament" title="SEC Tournament">
        SEC Tournament
       </a>
      </i>
      ) is the competition that determines the SEC's automatic bid to the
      <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">
       NCAA men's basketball tournament
      </a>
      . Notably, it does not determine the SEC conference champion in men's basketballthe conference has awarded its championship to the team(s) with the best regular-season record since the 195051 season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is a single-elimination tournament and seeding is based on regular season records.
     </p>
     <p>
      With the expansion to 14 members in 2012, the 2013 tournament was the first with a new format covering five days. The teams seeded 11 through 14 play on the first day, with the winners advancing to play the No. 5 and 6 seeds on Thursday. The top four teams receive a "double bye" and do not play until the quarterfinals on Friday.
     </p>
     <p>
      As of the most recently completed 201415 season, the tournament has most often been held at two venues that have each hosted 12 times.
      <a href="/wiki/Louisville_Gardens" title="Louisville Gardens">
       Louisville Gardens
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">
       Louisville, Kentucky
      </a>
      served as the regular host from 1941 until the tournament was discontinued after the 1952 edition. The
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Dome" title="Georgia Dome">
       Georgia Dome
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">
       Atlanta
      </a>
      first hosted the tournament in 1995 and most recently hosted in 2014.
      <a href="/wiki/Bridgestone_Arena" title="Bridgestone Arena">
       Bridgestone Arena
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">
       Nashville, Tennessee
      </a>
      is now the regular host, with that venue hosting the tournament from 2015 through 2025, except in 2018 and 2022 (years in which it will instead host the SEC women's basketball tournament).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nashville_2015-2025_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nashville_2015-2025-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sometimes, the tournament will take place at the
      <a href="/wiki/Smoothie_King_Center" title="Smoothie King Center">
       Smoothie King Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">
       New Orleans, Louisiana
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Amalie_Arena" title="Amalie Arena">
       Amalie Arena
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">
       Tampa, Florida
      </a>
      . During the two seasons in the 20152025 period in which Nashville will not host the men's tournament, it will be held at
      <a href="/wiki/Scottrade_Center" title="Scottrade Center">
       Scottrade Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">
       St. Louis, Missouri
      </a>
      in 2018 and Amalie Arena in 2022.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Prior to moving to the Georgia Dome, the tournament (during its modern, post-1979 era) was most often contested at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birmingham-Jefferson_Convention_Complex" title="Birmingham-Jefferson Convention Complex">
       Birmingham-Jefferson Civic Center
      </a>
      , home of the SEC's headquarters and centrally located prior to the addition of Arkansas and South Carolina. Other sites to host include on-campus arenas at LSU, Tennessee and Vanderbilt;
      <a href="/wiki/Rupp_Arena" title="Rupp Arena">
       Rupp Arena
      </a>
      in Lexington; and the
      <a href="/wiki/Amway_Arena" title="Amway Arena">
       Orlando Arena
      </a>
      .
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">
       Awards
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Awards">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEC_Men%27s_Basketball_Player_of_the_Year" title="SEC Men's Basketball Player of the Year">
       SEC Men's Basketball Player of the Year
      </a>
      is awarded to the player who has proven himself, throughout the season, to be the most exceptional talent in the Southeastern Conference. Various other awards, such as the best tournament player in the
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Men%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="SEC Men's Basketball Tournament">
       SEC Tournament
      </a>
      and all conference honors are given out throughout the year.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Baseball">
       Baseball
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Baseball">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEC_Baseball_Tournament" title="SEC Baseball Tournament">
       SEC Baseball Tournament
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Schools play a 30-game league schedule (10 three-game series). From 1996 through 2012, schools played all five schools within their division and five of the six schools from the opposite division. With the addition of Missouri and Texas A&amp;M, schools will now miss three opponents from the opposite division in a given season.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since 1990, the SEC has become the most successful conference on the college baseball diamond. That year, Georgia captured the conference's first national championship at the
      <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">
       College World Series
      </a>
      . Following that, LSU won 6 of the next 19 titles, including 5 of 10 between 1991 and 2000 and its sixth title in 2009. This was followed by South Carolina winning back to back titles in 2010 and 2011, and then by Vanderbilt winning its first title in 2014. During that same span, eight teams have also been runners-up at the CWS. In 1997 and 2011 the CWS final series involved two SEC teams. The only current SEC member that has never appeared in the CWS is
      <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_Wildcats_baseball" title="Kentucky Wildcats baseball">
       Kentucky
      </a>
      . Missouri and Texas A&amp;M have not made the CWS as SEC members, but both have made appearances while in other conferences. Both Georgia Tech and Tulane have made appearances in the CWS after leaving the SEC.
     </p>
     <p>
      SEC teams have also become leaders in total and average attendance over the years. In 2010 five of the top six drawing programs hailed from the SEC. Six more teams placed in the top 35 nationally.
     </p>
     <p>
      The NCAA automatic berth is given to the winner of the SEC Baseball Tournament, which was first started in 1977. It is a double-elimination tournament and seeding is based on regular season records. Since 1998, the tournament has been held at
      <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Metropolitan_Stadium" title="Hoover Metropolitan Stadium">
       Hoover Metropolitan Stadium
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Hoover,_Alabama" title="Hoover, Alabama">
       Hoover, Alabama
      </a>
      and contested under the format used at the
      <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">
       College World Series
      </a>
      from 1988 through 2002, with two four-team brackets leading to a single championship game. The winner receives the conference's automatic bid to the
      <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Championship" title="NCAA Division I Baseball Championship">
       NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      SEC presidents and athletic directors voted to expand the SEC Tournament to 10 teams starting in 2012. The division winners will receive a bye on the first day of competition, and the tournament will become single-elimination after the field is pared to four teams.
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to the winner of the SEC Baseball Tournament, the Southeastern Conference usually gets several at-large bids to the NCAA Tournament. Many teams have qualified for the NCAA Tournament despite failing to qualify for the SEC Tournament. One of those, Mississippi State, reached the College World Series in 2007.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="College_World_Series_champions.2C_runners-up_and_scores">
       College World Series champions, runners-up and scores
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: College World Series champions, runners-up and scores">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Note: Teams in
      <b>
       bold
      </b>
      are current SEC members who advanced to the CWS while in the conference. Teams in
      <i>
       <b>
        bold italics
       </b>
      </i>
      are current SEC members who were in another conference at the time of their appearance.
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rivalries_2">
       Rivalries
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Rivalries">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Several baseball rivalries have developed in the SEC:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/LSU_Tigers_baseball" title="LSU Tigers baseball">
        LSU
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Tulane_Green_Wave" title="Tulane Green Wave">
        Tulane
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Historically these schools were arch-rivals in all sports, but following Tulane's decades-long de-emphasis of sports, including its exit from the SEC in 1966, baseball is the only sport in which the two schools are relatively evenly matched. On several occasions match-ups between the two have drawn national record-setting attendances. Tulane reached its first
       <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">
        College World Series
       </a>
       in 2001 by defeating LSU in three games in the NCAA Super Regional. In 2002, the Tigers and Green Wave drew an NCAA regular season record crowd of 27,673 to the
       <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_Superdome" title="Mercedes-Benz Superdome">
        Louisiana Superdome
       </a>
       .
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/LSU_Tigers_baseball" title="LSU Tigers baseball">
        LSU
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_Bulldogs_baseball" title="Mississippi State Bulldogs baseball">
        Mississippi State
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Before the arrival of
       <a href="/wiki/Skip_Bertman" title="Skip Bertman">
        Skip Bertman
       </a>
       as LSU's baseball coach in 1984, Mississippi State had long dominated the conference in baseball, with most of that success coming under coach
       <a href="/wiki/Ron_Polk" title="Ron Polk">
        Ron Polk
       </a>
       , who returned to coach the Bulldogs in 2002 after retiring in 1997. When Bertman arrived in Baton Rouge, LSU's long-dormant program took off, winning 11 SEC championships and five
       <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">
        College World Series
       </a>
       championships between 1984 and 2001.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Gamecocks_baseball" title="South Carolina Gamecocks baseball">
        South Carolina
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Clemson_Tigers" title="Clemson Tigers">
        Clemson
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       This instate rivalry is an intense local affair, with the Gamecocks and Tigers meeting each regular season, and has gained national prominence as both teams are often ranked in the top 10 nationally. The highlights of the rivalry include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2002_College_World_Series" title="2002 College World Series">
        2002
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_College_World_Series" title="2010 College World Series">
        2010
       </a>
       meetings in the final four of the
       <a href="/wiki/College_World_Series" title="College World Series">
        College World Series
       </a>
       . Each time, South Carolina emerged from the losers bracket to beat Clemson twice and advance to the national championship series.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Gamecocks_baseball" title="South Carolina Gamecocks baseball">
        South Carolina
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels_baseball" title="North Carolina Tar Heels baseball">
        North Carolina
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       The Gamecocks and Tar Heels have met five times in the NCAA tournament between 2002 and 2013, including the
       <a href="/wiki/2002_NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Tournament" title="2002 NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament">
        2002 NCAA Regional
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2003_NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Tournament" title="2003 NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament">
        2003 NCAA Super Regional
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/2004_NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Tournament" title="2004 NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament">
        2004 NCAA Regional
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/2013_NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Tournament" title="2013 NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament">
        2013 NCAA Regional
       </a>
       , with the Gamecock's holding a 32 edge.
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Women.27s_basketball">
       Women's basketball
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Women's basketball">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The SEC has historically been the most dominant conference in women's basketball.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since the 200910 season, teams have played a 16-game conference schedule with a single league table; prior to that time the conference schedule was 14 games, again in a single table.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Like SEC men's basketball, women's basketball used the divisional alignment for scheduling purposes through the 201112 season; however, the women's scheduling format was significantly different from the men's. Each team played home-and-home games against five schoolsone permanent opponent, two teams from the same division, and two teams from the opposite division; the non-permanent home-and-home opponents rotated every two years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The remaining games were single games against the six other schools in the conference, with three at home and three away.
     </p>
     <p>
      The league voted to keep a 16-game league schedule even after the addition of Missouri and Texas A&amp;M. Arkansas and LSU are no longer permanent opponents, with the Razorbacks picking up Missouri and the Tigers picking up Texas A&amp;M. The other permanent opponents are the same as men's basketball, except for Florida-Georgia and Kentucky-South Carolina (both pairs had been permanent women's basketball opponents before the 2012 expansion). Each school plays two others home-and-home during a given season and the other 10 once each. The divisional alignments no longer play any role in scheduling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The recent history of SEC women's basketball is dominated by
      <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Lady_Volunteers_basketball" title="Tennessee Lady Volunteers basketball">
       Tennessee
      </a>
      , who have won regular season and/or conference tournament championships in 20 of the last 22 seasons, as well as 8 national championships since 1987. In the 28 seasons the
      <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Women%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Women's Division I Basketball Championship">
       NCAA Women's Division I Basketball Championship
      </a>
      has been held, SEC schools have reached the Final Four 32 times, more than twice as often as any other conference.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Basketball_tournament_2">
       Basketball tournament
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Southeastern_Conference&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Basketball tournament">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/SEC_Women%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="SEC Women's Basketball Tournament">
       SEC Women's Basketball Tournament
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/SEC_Women%27s_Basketball_Tournament" title="SEC Women's Basketball Tournament">
        SEC Women's Basketball Tournament
       </a>
      </i>
      is currently held a week before the men's basketball tournament. Like the men's version, it is a single-elimination tournament involving all conference members, with seeding based on regular season records. With the expansion to 14 schools, the bottom four teams in the conference standings play opening-round games, and the top four receive "double byes" into the quarterfinals. The winner earns the conference's automatic bid to the
      <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Women%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Women's Division I Basketball Championship">
       NCAA women's basketball tournament
      </a>
      . Also paralleling the men's tournament, the women's tournament does not determine the SEC champion; that honor has been awarded based on regular-season record since the 198586 season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The tournament, inaugurated in 1980, was originally held on campus sites; the first tournament to take place at a neutral site was in 1987. The two most frequent sites for the tournament have been
      <a href="/wiki/McKenzie_Arena" title="McKenzie Arena">
       McKenzie Arena
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga,_Tennessee" title="Chattanooga, Tennessee">
       Chattanooga, Tennessee
      </a>
      (seven times) and the
      <a href="/wiki/Albany_Civic_Center" title="Albany Civic Center">
       Albany Civic Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Albany,_Georgia" title="Albany, Georgia">
       Albany, Georgia
      </a>
      (six times); however, the tournament was last played in Albany in 1992 and Chattanooga in 2000. Because demand for women's tournament tickets is generally lower than for the men's tournament, it is typically played in a smaller venue than the men's tournament in the same season. The most frequent venues since 2000 have been Bridgestone Arena in Nashville (five times), the
      <a href="/wiki/Arena_at_Gwinnett_Center" title="Arena at Gwinnett Center">
       Arena at Gwinnett Center
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Duluth,_Georgia" title="Duluth, Georgia">
       Duluth, Georgia
      </a>
      (four), and
      <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Arena" title="Verizon Arena">
       Verizon Arena
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/North_Little_Rock,_Arkansas" title="North Little Rock, Arkansas">
       North Little Rock, Arkansas
      </a>
      (four).
     </p>
